<div id="Greco-Persian_Wars" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><strong class="selflink">Greco-Persian Wars</strong></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">First Persian invasion</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Greek_counterattack_.28479.E2.80.93478_BC.29" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greek counterattack</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Wars of the Delian League">Wars of the Delian League</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>The <b>Greco-Persian Wars</b> (also often called the <b>Persian Wars</b>) were a series of conflicts between the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">Greek city-states</a> that started in 499 BC<sup class="reference" id="ref_i"><a href="#cnote_i">i[›]</a></sup> and lasted until 449 BC. The collision between the fractious political world of the Greeks and the enormous empire of the Persians began when <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> conquered the Greek-inhabited region of <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> in 547 BC. Struggling to rule the independent-minded cities of Ionia, the Persians appointed <a href="/wiki/Tyrant#Historical_forms" title="Tyrant">tyrants</a> to rule each of them. This would prove to be the source of much trouble for the Greeks and Persians alike.</p>
<p>In 499 BC, the tyrant of <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristagoras" title="Aristagoras">Aristagoras</a>, embarked on an <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naxos_(499_BC)" title="Siege of Naxos (499 BC)">expedition</a> to conquer the island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naxos_Island" title="Naxos Island">Naxos</a>, with Persian support;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> however, the expedition was a debacle and, pre-empting his dismissal, Aristagoras incited all of Hellenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> into rebellion against the Persians. This was the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a>, which would last until 493 BC, progressively drawing more regions of Asia Minor into the conflict. Aristagoras secured military support from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eretria" title="Eretria">Eretria</a>, and in 498 BC these forces helped to capture and burn the Persian regional capital of <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>. The Persian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius the Great</a> vowed to have revenge on Athens and Eretria for this act. The revolt continued, with the two sides effectively stalemated throughout 497–495 BC. In 494 BC, the Persians regrouped, and attacked the epicentre of the revolt in Miletus. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lade" title="Battle of Lade">Battle of Lade</a>, the Ionians suffered a decisive defeat, and the rebellion collapsed, with the final members being stamped out the following year.</p>
<p>Seeking to secure his empire from further revolts and from the interference of the mainland Greeks, Darius embarked on a scheme to conquer Greece and to punish Athens and Eretria for the burning of Sardis. The <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">first Persian invasion of Greece</a> began in 492 BC, with the Persian general <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a> successfully re-subjugating <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and conquering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> before several mishaps forced an early end to the rest of the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138-4">[4]</a></sup> In 490 BC a second force was sent to Greece, this time across the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, under the command of <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">Datis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes_(son_of_Artaphernes)" title="Artaphernes (son of Artaphernes)">Artaphernes</a>. This expedition subjugated the <a href="/wiki/Cyclades" title="Cyclades">Cyclades</a>, before <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Eretria" title="Siege of Eretria">besieging, capturing and razing Eretria</a>. However, while en route to attack Athens, the Persian force was decisively defeated by the Athenians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a>, ending Persian efforts for the time being.</p>
<p>Darius then began to plan to completely conquer Greece, but died in 486 BC and responsibility for the conquest passed to his son <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes</a>. In 480 BC, Xerxes personally led the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">second Persian invasion of Greece</a> with one of the largest ancient armies ever assembled. Victory over the Allied Greek states at the famous <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Battle of Thermopylae</a> allowed the Persians to torch an evacuated Athens and overrun most of Greece. However, while seeking to destroy the combined Greek fleet, the Persians suffered a severe defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>. The following year, the confederated Greeks went on the offensive, defeating the Persian army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>, and ending the invasion of Greece.</p>
<p>The allied Greeks followed up their success by destroying the rest of the Persian fleet at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Battle of Mycale</a>, before expelling Persian garrisons from <a href="/wiki/Sestos" title="Sestos">Sestos</a> (479 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> (478 BC). The actions of the general <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">Pausanias</a> at the siege of Byzantium alienated many of the Greek states from the Spartans, and the anti-Persian alliance was therefore reconstituted around Athenian leadership, called the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a>. The Delian League <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Wars of the Delian League">continued to campaign</a> against Persia for the next three decades, beginning with the expulsion of the remaining Persian garrisons from <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Eurymedon" title="Battle of the Eurymedon">Battle of the Eurymedon</a> in 466 BC, the League won a double victory that finally secured freedom for the cities of Ionia. However, the League's involvement in an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> revolt (from 460–454 BC) resulted in a disastrous defeat, and further campaigning was suspended. A fleet was sent to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> in 451 BC, but achieved little, and when it withdrew the Greco-Persian Wars drew to a quiet end. Some historical sources suggest the end of hostilities was marked by a peace treaty between Athens and Persia, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Callias" title="Peace of Callias">Peace of Callias</a>.</p>
<p></p>

</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1714" data-file-width="1200" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/180px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/270px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/360px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, the main historical source for this conflict</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1735" data-file-width="998" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/180px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/270px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/360px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> continued Herodotus's narrative</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Almost all the primary sources for the Greco-Persian Wars are Greek; there are no surviving historical accounts from the Persian side. By some distance, the main source for the Greco-Persian Wars is the Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>. Herodotus, who has been called the "Father of History",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> was born in 484 BC in <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a>, Asia Minor (then part of the Persian empire). He wrote his 'Enquiries' (Greek <i>Historia</i>, English <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">(The) Histories</a></i>) around 440–430 BC, trying to trace the origins of the Greco-Persian Wars, which would still have been recent history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-6">[6]</a></sup> Herodotus's approach was novel and, at least in Western society, he invented 'history' as a discipline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-6">[6]</a></sup> As Holland has it: "For the first time, a chronicler set himself to trace the origins of a conflict not to a past so remote so as to be utterly fabulous, nor to the whims and wishes of some god, nor to a people's claim to manifest destiny, but rather explanations he could verify personally."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some later ancient historians, starting with <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>, criticised Herodotus and his methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin15_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fin15-8">[8]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Thucydides chose to begin his history where Herodotus left off (at the <a href="#Sestos">Siege of Sestos</a>) and felt Herodotus's history was accurate enough not to need re-writing or correcting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin15_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fin15-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> criticised Herodotus in his essay "On The Malignity of Herodotus", describing Herodotus as "<i>Philobarbaros</i>" (barbarian-lover) for not being pro-Greek enough, which suggests that Herodotus might actually have done a reasonable job of being even-handed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> A negative view of Herodotus was passed on to Renaissance Europe, though he remained well read. However, since the 19th century his reputation has been dramatically rehabilitated by archaeological finds that have repeatedly confirmed his version of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h377_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-h377-10">[10]</a></sup> The prevailing modern view is that Herodotus did a remarkable job in his <i>Historia</i>, but that some of his specific details (particularly troop numbers and dates) should be viewed with skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h377_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-h377-10">[10]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are still some historians who believe Herodotus made up much of his story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The military history of Greece between the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">second Persian invasion of Greece</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> (479–431 BC) is not well supported by surviving ancient sources. This period, sometimes referred to as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentekontaetia" title="Pentekontaetia">pentekontaetia</a></i> (<i>πεντηκονταετία</i>, <i>the Fifty Years</i>) by ancient writers, was a period of relative peace and prosperity within Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin16_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fin16-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kag77_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kag77-13">[13]</a></sup> The richest source for the period, and also the most contemporaneous, is Thucydides' <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Peloponnesian_War" title="History of the Peloponnesian War">History of the Peloponnesian War</a></i>, which is generally considered by modern historians to be a reliable primary account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea264_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea264-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine336_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine336-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin29_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fin29-16">[16]</a></sup> Thucydides only mentions this period in a digression on the growth of Athenian power in the run up to the Peloponnesian War, and the account is brief, probably selective and lacks any dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea248.2A_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea248.2A-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine343_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine343-18">[18]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Thucydides's account can be, and is, used by historians to draw up a skeleton chronology for the period, on to which details from archaeological records and other writers can be superimposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea248.2A_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sea248.2A-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>More detail for the whole period is provided by Plutarch, in his <a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">biographies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristides" title="Aristides">Aristides</a> and especially <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a>. Plutarch was writing some 600 years after the events in question, and is therefore a secondary source, but he often names his sources, which allows some degree of verification of his statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In his biographies, he draws directly from many ancient histories that have not survived, and thus often preserves details of the period that are omitted in Herodotus and Thucydides's accounts. The final major existing source for the period is the universal history (<i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">Bibliotheca historica</a></i>) of the 1st century BC Sicilian, <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>. Much of Diodorus's writing about this period is drawn from the much earlier Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a>, who also wrote a universal history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine360_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-20">[20]</a></sup> Diodorus is also a secondary source and often derided by modern historians for his style and inaccuracies, but he preserves many details of the ancient period found nowhere else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further scattered details can be found in <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>'s <i>Description of Greece</i>, while the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> dictionary of the 10th century AD preserves some anecdotes found nowhere else. Minor sources for the period include the works of <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Trogus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Trogus">Pompeius Trogus</a> (epitomized by <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justinus</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Nepos" title="Cornelius Nepos">Cornelius Nepos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias of Cnidus</a> (epitomized by <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photius</a>), which are not in their original textual form. These works are not considered reliable (especially Ctesias), and are not particularly useful for reconstructing the history of this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_conflict">Origins of the conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins of the conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Greeks of the classical period believed that, in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">dark age</a> that followed the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a>, significant numbers of Greeks fled and had emigrated to Asia Minor and settled there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I14x_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-I14x-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Modern historians generally accept this migration as historic (but separate from the later colonization of the Mediterranean by the Greeks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> There are, however, those who believe the Ionian migration cannot be explained as simply as the classical Greeks claimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> These settlers were from three tribal groups: the <a href="/wiki/Aeolians" title="Aeolians">Aeolians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I14x_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-I14x-24">[24]</a></sup> The Ionians had settled about the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, founding the twelve cities that made up <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I14x_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-I14x-24">[24]</a></sup> These cities were <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myus" title="Myus">Myus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a> in Caria; <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colophon_(city)" title="Colophon (city)">Colophon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lebedos" title="Lebedos">Lebedos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teos" title="Teos">Teos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clazomenae" title="Clazomenae">Clazomenae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phocaea" title="Phocaea">Phocaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erythrae" title="Erythrae">Erythrae</a> in Lydia; and the islands of <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I142_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-I142-29">[29]</a></sup> Although the Ionian cities were independent of one another, they recognized their shared heritage and supposedly had a common temple and meeting place, the <i>Panionion</i>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_ii"><a href="#cnote_ii">ii[›]</a></sup> They thus formed a 'cultural league', to which they would admit no other cities, or even other tribal Ionians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I143_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-I143-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I148_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-I148-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cities of Ionia remained independent until they were conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Lydians" title="Lydians">Lydians</a> of western Asia Minor. The Lydian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alyattes_II" title="Alyattes II">Alyattes II</a> attacked Miletus, a conflict that ended with a treaty of alliance between Miletus and Lydia, that meant that Miletus would have internal autonomy but follow Lydia in foreign affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> At this time, the Lydians were also in conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median</a> Empire, and the Milesians sent an army to aid the Lydians in this conflict. Eventually a peaceable settlement was established between the Medes and the Lydians, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halys_River" title="Halys River">Halys River</a> set up as the border between the kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The famous Lydian king <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">Croesus</a> succeeded his father Alyattes in around 560 BC and set about conquering the other Greek city states of Asia Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I126_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-I126-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus</a> led a rebellion against the last Median king <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">Astyages</a> in 553 BC. Cyrus was a grandson of Astyages and was supported by part of the Median aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h9_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-h9-35">[35]</a></sup> By 550 BC, the rebellion was over, and Cyrus had emerged victorious, founding the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in place of the Median kingdom in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h9_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-h9-35">[35]</a></sup> Croesus saw the disruption in the Median Empire and Persia as an opportunity to extend his realm and asked the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delphic_Oracle" title="Delphic Oracle">oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a> whether he should attack them. The Oracle supposedly replied the famously ambiguous answer that "if Croesus was to cross the Halys he would destroy a great empire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Blind to the ambiguity of this prophecy, Croesus attacked the Persians, but was eventually defeated and Lydia fell to Cyrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> By crossing the Halys, Croesus had indeed destroyed a great empire – his own.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="1020" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png/300px-AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png/450px-AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png/600px-AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AchaemenidMapBehistunInscription.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Achaemenid Empire at its greatest extent listed on the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While fighting the Lydians, Cyrus had sent messages to the Ionians asking them to revolt against Lydian rule, which the Ionians had refused to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I141_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-I141-38">[38]</a></sup> After Cyrus finished the conquest of Lydia, the Ionian cities now offered to be his subjects under the same terms as they had been subjects of Croesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I141_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-I141-38">[38]</a></sup> Cyrus refused, citing the Ionians' unwillingness to help him previously. The Ionians thus prepared to defend themselves, and Cyrus sent the Median general <a href="/wiki/Harpagus" title="Harpagus">Harpagus</a> to conquer them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I163_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-I163-39">[39]</a></sup> He first attacked Phocaea; the Phocaeans decided to abandon their city entirely and sail into exile in Sicily, rather than become Persian subjects (although many later returned).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I164_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-I164-40">[40]</a></sup> Some Teians also chose to emigrate when Harpagus attacked Teos, but the rest of the Ionians remained, and were each in turn conquered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I169_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-I169-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the years following their conquest, the Persians found the Ionians difficult to rule. Elsewhere in the empire, Cyrus identified elite native groups such as the priesthood of Judea – to help him rule his new subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h147_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-h147-42">[42]</a></sup> No such group existed in Greek cities at this time; while there was usually an aristocracy, this was inevitably divided into feuding factions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h147_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-h147-42">[42]</a></sup> The Persians thus settled for sponsoring a tyrant in each Ionian city, even though this drew them into the Ionians' internal conflicts. Furthermore, certain tyrants might develop an independent streak and have to be replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h147_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-h147-42">[42]</a></sup> The tyrants themselves faced a difficult task; they had to deflect the worst of their fellow citizens' hatred, while staying in the favour of the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h147_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-h147-42">[42]</a></sup> In the past, Greek states had often been ruled by tyrants, but that form of government was on the decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-fine-43">[43]</a></sup> Past tyrants had also tended and needed to be strong and able leaders, whereas the rulers appointed by the Persians were simply place-men. Backed by Persian military might, these tyrants did not need the support of the population, and could thus rule absolutely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-fine-43">[43]</a></sup> On the eve of the Greco-Persian wars, it is probable that the Ionian population had become discontented and was ready for rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h155_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-h155-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare_in_the_ancient_Mediterranean">Warfare in the ancient Mediterranean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Warfare in the ancient Mediterranean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Greco-Persian wars both sides made use of spear-armed infantry and light missile troops. Greek armies placed the emphasis on heavier infantry, while Persian armies favoured lighter troop types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-L23-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia">Persia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Persia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1107" data-file-width="1675" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/300px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/450px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/600px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian (and Median) warriors</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Persian military consisted of a diverse group of men drawn across the various nations of the empire. However, according to Herodotus, there was at least a general conformity in armour and style of fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-L23-45">[45]</a></sup> The troops were usually armed with a bow, a 'short spear' and a sword or axe, carried a wicker shield. They wore a leather jerkin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-L23-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> although individuals of high stature wore high quality metal armor. The Persians most likely used their bows to wear down the enemy, then closed in to deliver the final blow with spears and swords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-L23-45">[45]</a></sup> The first rank of Persian infantry formations, the so-called '<a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">sparabara</a>', had no bows, carried larger wicker shields and were sometimes armed with longer spears. Their role was to protect the back ranks of the formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The cavalry probably fought as lightly armed missile cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-L23-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The style of warfare between the Greek city-states, which dates back until at least 650 BC (as dated by the '<a href="/wiki/Chigi_vase" title="Chigi vase">Chigi vase</a>'), was based around the <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">phalanx</a> supported by missile troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h69_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-h69-50">[50]</a></sup> The '<a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplites</a>' were foot soldiers usually drawn from the members of the middle-classes (in Athens called the <i>zeugites</i>), who could afford the equipment necessary to fight in this manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The heavy armour usually included a breastplate or a <a href="/wiki/Linothorax" title="Linothorax">linothorax</a>, greaves, a helmet, and a large round, concave shield (the <i><a href="/wiki/Aspis" title="Aspis">aspis</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoplon" title="Hoplon">hoplon</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup> Hoplites were armed with long spears (the <i><a href="/wiki/Dory_(spear)" title="Dory (spear)">dory</a></i>), which were significantly longer than Persian spears, and a sword (the <i><a href="/wiki/Xiphos" title="Xiphos">xiphos</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup> The heavy armour and longer spears made them superior in hand-to-hand combat<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup> and gave them significant protection against ranged attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_46-5"><a href="#cite_note-L256-46">[46]</a></sup> Lightly armed skirmishers, the <a href="/wiki/Psiloi" title="Psiloi">psiloi</a> also comprised a part of Greek armies growing in importance during the conflict; at the Battle of Plataea, for instance, they may have formed over half the Greek army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Use of cavalry in Greek armies is not reported in the battles of the Greco-Persian Wars.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_warfare">Naval warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Naval warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the beginning of the conflict, all naval forces in the eastern Mediterranean had switched to the <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">trireme</a>, a warship powered by three banks of oars. The most common naval tactics during the period were ramming (Greek triremes were equipped with a cast-bronze ram at the bows), or boarding by ship-borne marines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L34_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-L34-53">[53]</a></sup> More experienced naval powers had by this time also begun to use a manoeuver known as <i>diekplous</i>. It is not clear what this was, but it probably involved sailing into gaps between enemy ships and then ramming them in the side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L34_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-L34-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Persian naval forces were primarily provided by the seafaring people of the empire: <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Cyprus" title="Ancient history of Cyprus">Cypriots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII89_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-VII89-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI9_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI9-55">[55]</a></sup> Other coastal regions of the Persian Empire would contribute ships throughout the course of the wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII89_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-VII89-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ionian_Revolt_.28499.E2.80.93493_BC.29">Ionian Revolt (499–493 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ionian Revolt (499–493 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a></div>
<div aria-labelledby="Ionian_Revolt" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">
<table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit">
<tr>
<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt" title="Template:Campaignbox Ionian Revolt"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Ionian Revolt"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="Ionian_Revolt" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naxos_(499_BC)" title="Siege of Naxos (499 BC)">Naxos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Sardis" title="Ionian Revolt">Sardis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Ephesus" title="Ionian Revolt">Ephesus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Cyprus" title="Ionian Revolt">Cyprus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_the_Marsyas" title="Ionian Revolt">Marsyas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Labraunda" title="Ionian Revolt">Labraunda</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Pedasus" title="Ionian Revolt">Pedasus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lade" title="Battle of Lade">Lade</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Fall_of_Miletus" title="Ionian Revolt">Miletus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Chios" title="Ionian Revolt">Chios</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Malene" title="Ionian Revolt">Malene</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> and associated revolts in <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doris_(Asia_Minor)" title="Doris (Asia Minor)">Doris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a> were military rebellions by several regions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> against Persian rule, lasting from 499 to 493 BC. At the heart of the rebellion was the dissatisfaction of the Greek cities of Asia Minor with the tyrants appointed by Persia to rule them, along with opposition to the individual actions of two Milesian tyrants, <a href="/wiki/Histiaeus" title="Histiaeus">Histiaeus</a> and Aristagoras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h147_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-h147-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h153_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-h153-56">[56]</a></sup> In 499 BC the then tyrant of Miletus, Aristagoras, launched a joint expedition with the Persian satrap <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a> to conquer Naxos, in an attempt to bolster his position in Miletus (both financially and in terms of prestige).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h153_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-h153-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V31_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-V31-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naxos_(499_BC)" title="Siege of Naxos (499 BC)">mission</a> was a debacle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V33_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-V33-58">[58]</a></sup> and sensing his imminent removal as tyrant, Aristagoras chose to incite the whole of Ionia into rebellion against the Persian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h155_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-h155-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1645" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/200px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/300px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/400px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing main events of the Ionian Revolt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Struggling to rule the independent-minded cities of Ionia, the Persians appointed local <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrants</a> to rule each of them. This would prove to be the source of much trouble for the Greeks and Persians alike. In 498 BC, supported by troops from Athens and Eretria, the Ionians marched on, captured, and burnt Sardis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V100.2A_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-V100.2A-59">[59]</a></sup> However, on their return journey to Ionia, they were followed by Persian troops, and decisively beaten at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ephesus_(498_BC)" title="Battle of Ephesus (498 BC)">Battle of Ephesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V102_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-V102-60">[60]</a></sup> This campaign was the only offensive action taken by the Ionians, who subsequently went on the defensive. The Persians responded in 497 BC with a three-pronged attack aimed at recapturing the outlying areas of the rebellious territory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V116_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-V116-61">[61]</a></sup> but the spread of the revolt to Caria meant the largest army, under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius</a>, moved there instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V117_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-V117-62">[62]</a></sup> While at first campaigning successfully in Caria, this army was wiped out in an ambush at the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Pedasus" title="Ionian Revolt">Battle of Pedasus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V121_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-V121-63">[63]</a></sup> This resulted in a stalemate for the rest of 496 and 495 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-board-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 494 BC the Persian army and navy had regrouped, and they made straight for the epicentre of the rebellion at Miletus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI6_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI6-65">[65]</a></sup> The Ionian fleet sought to defend Miletus by sea, but was defeated decisively at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lade" title="Battle of Lade">Battle of Lade</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samians</a> had defected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI8_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI8-66">[66]</a></sup> Miletus was then besieged, captured, and its population was enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI19_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI19-67">[67]</a></sup> This double defeat effectively ended the revolt, and the Carians surrendered to the Persians as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI25_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI25-68">[68]</a></sup> The Persians spent 493 BC reducing the cities along the west coast that still held out against them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI31_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI31-69">[69]</a></sup> before finally imposing a peace settlement on Ionia that was considered<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (June 2011)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to be both just and fair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h175_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-h175-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ionian Revolt constituted the first major conflict between Greece and the Achaemenid Empire and represents the first phase of the Greco-Persian Wars. Asia Minor had been brought back into the Persian fold, but Darius had vowed to punish Athens and Eretria for their support for the revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h175_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-h175-70">[70]</a></sup> Moreover, seeing that the political situation in Greece posed a continued threat to the stability of his Empire, he decided to embark on the conquest of all Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h175_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-h175-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_invasion_of_Greece_.28492.E2.80.93490_BC.29">First invasion of Greece (492–490 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: First invasion of Greece (492–490 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">First Persian invasion of Greece</a></div>
<div aria-labelledby="First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">
<table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit">
<tr>
<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template:Campaignbox First Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template talk:Campaignbox First Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>

<div id="First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">First Persian invasion<br/>
of Greece</a></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>

<li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Naxos" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">Naxos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Karystos" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">Karystos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Eretria" title="Siege of Eretria">Eretria</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Marathon</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>After having reconquered Ionia, the Persians began to plan their next moves of extinguishing the threat to their empire from Greece; and punishing Athens and Eretria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h177_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-h177-71">[71]</a></sup> The resultant <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">first Persian invasion of Greece</a> consisted of two main campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h177_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-h177-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="492_BC:_Mardonius.27s_campaign">492 BC: Mardonius's campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 492 BC: Mardonius's campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="992" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/300px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/450px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/600px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing events of the first phases of the Greco-Persian Wars</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first campaign, in 492 BC, was led by Darius's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI43_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI43-72">[72]</a></sup> who re-subjugated <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, which had nominally been part of the Persian empire since 513 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Mardonius was also able to force <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> to become a fully subordinate client kingdom of Persia; it had previously been a <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a>, but retained a broad degree of autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI44_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI44-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011343_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011343-75">[75]</a></sup> However, further progress in this campaign was prevented when Mardonius's fleet was wrecked in a storm off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a>. Mardonius himself was then injured in a raid on his camp by a Thracian tribe, and after this he returned with the rest of the expedition to Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI44_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-VI44-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI45_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI45-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following year, having given clear warning of his plans, Darius sent ambassadors to all the cities of Greece, demanding their submission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI48_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI48-77">[77]</a></sup> He received it from almost all of them, except Athens and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, both of whom instead executed the ambassadors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI48_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-VI48-77">[77]</a></sup> With Athens still defiant, and Sparta now also effectively at war with him, Darius ordered a further military campaign for the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h181_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-h181-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="490_BC:_Datis_and_Artaphernes.27_campaign">490 BC: Datis and Artaphernes' campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 490 BC: Datis and Artaphernes' campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 490 BC, <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">Datis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes_(son_of_Artaphernes)" title="Artaphernes (son of Artaphernes)">Artaphernes</a> (son of the satrap <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a>) were given command of an amphibious invasion force, and set sail from <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h181_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-h181-78">[78]</a></sup> The Persian force sailed first to the island of <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, where a Lindian Temple Chronicle records that <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">Datis</a> besieged the city of <a href="/wiki/Lindos" title="Lindos">Lindos</a>, but was unsuccessful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The fleet sailed next to Naxos, to punish the Naxians for their resistance to the failed expedition the Persians had mounted there a decade earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h183_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-h183-80">[80]</a></sup> Many of the inhabitants fled to the mountains; those that the Persians caught were enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI96_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI96-81">[81]</a></sup> The Persians then burnt the city and temples of the Naxians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI96_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-VI96-81">[81]</a></sup> The fleet then proceeded to island-hop across the rest of the Aegean on its way to Eretria, taking hostages and troops from each island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h183_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-h183-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The task force sailed on to <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>, and to the first major target, Eretria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI100_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI100-82">[82]</a></sup> The Eretrians made no attempt to stop the Persians from landing or advancing and thus allowed themselves to be <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Eretria" title="Siege of Eretria">besieged</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI101_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI101-83">[83]</a></sup> For six days, the Persians attacked the walls, with losses on both sides;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI101_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-VI101-83">[83]</a></sup> however, on the seventh day two reputable Eretrians opened the gates and betrayed the city to the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI101_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-VI101-83">[83]</a></sup> The city was razed, and temples and shrines were looted and burned. Furthermore, according to Darius's commands, the Persians enslaved all the remaining townspeople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI101_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-VI101-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Marathon">Battle of Marathon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Battle of Marathon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="554" data-file-width="704" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/250px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/375px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/500px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Greek wings envelop the Persians</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Persian fleet next headed south down the coast of <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a>, landing at the bay of <a href="/wiki/Marathon,_Greece" title="Marathon, Greece">Marathon</a>, roughly 25 miles (40 km) from Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Under the guidance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miltiades_the_Younger" title="Miltiades the Younger">Miltiades</a>, the general with the greatest experience of fighting the Persians, the Athenian army marched to block the two exits from the plain of Marathon. Stalemate ensued for five days, before the Persians decided to continue onward to Athens, and began to load their troops back onto the ships. After the Persians had loaded their cavalry (their strongest soldiers) on the ships, the 10,000 Athenian soldiers descended from the hills around the plain. The Greeks crushed the weaker Persian foot soldiers by routing the wings before turning towards the centre of the Persian line. The remnants of the Persian army fled to their ships and left the battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h195_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-h195-85">[85]</a></sup> Herodotus records that 6,400 Persian bodies were counted on the battlefield; the Athenians lost only 192 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VI117_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-VI117-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>As soon as the Persian survivors had put to sea, the Athenians marched as quickly as possible to Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> They arrived in time to prevent Artaphernes from securing a landing in Athens. Seeing his opportunity lost, Artaphernes ended the year's campaign and returned to Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Battle of Marathon was a watershed in the Greco-Persian wars, showing the Greeks that the Persians could be beaten. It also highlighted the superiority of the more heavily armoured Greek hoplites, and showed their potential when used wisely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h195_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-h195-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interbellum_.28490.E2.80.93480_BC.29">Interbellum (490–480 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Interbellum (490–480 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Empire">Achaemenid Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Achaemenid Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the failure of the first invasion, Darius began raising a huge new army with which he intended to subjugate Greece completely. However, in 486 BC, his <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> subjects revolted, and the revolt forced an indefinite postponement of any Greek expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h202_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-h202-89">[89]</a></sup> Darius died while preparing to march on Egypt, and the throne of Persia passed to his son <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Xerxes crushed the Egyptian revolt, and very quickly resumed the preparations for the invasion of Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h208_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-h208-91">[91]</a></sup> Since this was to be a full-scale invasion, it needed longterm planning, stockpiling and conscription. Xerxes decided that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">Hellespont</a> would be bridged to allow his army to cross to Europe, and that a canal should be dug across the isthmus of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> (a Persian fleet had been destroyed in 492 BC while rounding this coastline). These were both feats of exceptional ambition that would have been beyond the capabilities of any other contemporary state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h213_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-h213-92">[92]</a></sup> However, the campaign was delayed by one year because of another revolt in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Persians had the sympathy of several Greek city-states, including <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">Argos</a>, which had pledged to defect when the Persians reached their borders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aleuadae" title="Aleuadae">Aleuadae</a> family, who ruled <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, saw the invasion as an opportunity to extend their power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, though not explicitly 'Medising', was suspected of being willing to aid the Persians once the invasion force arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h263_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-h263-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 481 BC, after roughly four years of preparation, Xerxes began to muster the troops to invade Europe. Herodotus gives the names of 46 nations from which troops were drafted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The Persian army was gathered in Asia Minor in the summer and autumn of 481 BC. The armies from the Eastern satrapies were gathered in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kritala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kritala (page does not exist)">Kritala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a> and were led by Xerxes to Sardis where they passed the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Early in spring, it moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abydos,_Hellespont" title="Abydos, Hellespont">Abydos</a> where it was joined with the armies of the western satrapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Then the army that Xerxes had mustered marched towards Europe, crossing the Hellespont on two <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_the_Persian_forces">Size of the Persian forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Size of the Persian forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Size_of_the_Persian_forces" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece § Size of the Persian forces</a></div>
<p>The numbers of troops that Xerxes mustered for the second invasion of Greece have been the subject of endless dispute. Most modern scholars reject as unrealistic the figures of 2.5 million given by Herodotus and other ancient sources because the victors likely miscalculated or exaggerated. The topic has been hotly debated, but the consensus revolves around the figure of 200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-souza_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-souza-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The size of the Persian fleet is also disputed, although perhaps less so. Other ancient authors agree with Herodotus' number of 1,207. These numbers are by ancient standards consistent, and this could be interpreted that a number around 1,200 is correct. Among modern scholars, some have accepted this number, although suggesting the number must have been lower by the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L93_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-L93-105">[105]</a></sup> Other recent works on the Persian Wars reject this number, viewing 1,207 as more of a reference to the combined Greek fleet in the <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a>. These works generally claim that the Persians could have launched no more than around 600 warships into the Aegean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L93_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-L93-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_city_states">Greek city states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Greek city states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Athens">Athens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Athens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A year after Marathon, Miltiades, the hero of Marathon, was injured in a military campaign to <a href="/wiki/Paros" title="Paros">Paros</a>. Taking advantage of his incapacitation, the powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcmaeonid" title="Alcmaeonid">Alcmaeonid</a> family arranged for him to be prosecuted for the failure of the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h214_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-h214-108">[108]</a></sup> A huge fine was imposed on Miltiades for the crime of 'deceiving the Athenian people', but he died weeks later from his wound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h214_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-h214-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The politician <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, with a power base firmly established amongst the poor, filled the vacuum left by Miltiades's death, and in the following decade became the most influential politician in Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h214_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-h214-108">[108]</a></sup> During this period, Themistocles continued to support the expansion of Athens' naval power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h214_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-h214-108">[108]</a></sup> The Athenians were aware throughout this period that the Persian interest in Greece had not ended,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h208_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-h208-91">[91]</a></sup> and Themistocles's naval policies may be seen in the light of the potential threat from Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h214_108-4"><a href="#cite_note-h214-108">[108]</a></sup> Aristides, Themistocles's great rival, and champion of the <i>zeugites</i> (the 'upper hoplite-class') vigorously opposed such a policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h217_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-h217-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 483 BC, a vast new seam of silver was found in the Athenian mines at <a href="/wiki/Laurium" title="Laurium">Laurium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT4_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT4-110">[110]</a></sup> Themistocles proposed that the silver should be used to build a new fleet of triremes, ostensibly to assist in a long running war with <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aegina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h219_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-h219-111">[111]</a></sup> Plutarch suggests that Themistocles deliberately avoided mentioning Persia, believing that it was too distant a threat for the Athenians to act on, but that countering Persia was the fleet's aim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT4_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-PT4-110">[110]</a></sup> Fine suggests that many Athenians must have admitted that such a fleet would be needed to resist the Persians, whose preparations for the coming campaign were known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine292_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-fine292-112">[112]</a></sup> Themistocles's motion was passed easily, despite strong opposition from Aristides. Its passage was probably due to the desire of many of the poorer Athenians for paid employment as rowers in the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine292_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-fine292-112">[112]</a></sup> It is unclear from the ancient sources whether 100 or 200 ships were initially authorised; both Fine and Holland suggest that at first 100 ships were authorised and that a second vote increased this number to the levels seen during the second invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h219_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-h219-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine292_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-fine292-112">[112]</a></sup> Aristides continued to oppose Themistocles's policy, and tension between the two camps built over the winter, so the <a href="/wiki/Ostracism" title="Ostracism">ostracism</a> of 482 BC became a direct contest between Themistocles and Aristides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h219_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-h219-111">[111]</a></sup> In what Holland characterises as, in essence, the world's first referendum, Aristides was ostracised, and Themistocles's policies were endorsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h219_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-h219-111">[111]</a></sup> Indeed, becoming aware of the Persian preparations for the coming invasion, the Athenians voted to build more ships than those for which Themistocles had asked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h219_111-4"><a href="#cite_note-h219-111">[111]</a></sup> Thus, during the preparations for the Persian invasion, Themistocles had become the leading politician in Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT5_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT5-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sparta">Sparta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sparta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Demaratus" title="Demaratus">Demaratus</a> had been stripped of his kingship in 491 BC, and replaced with his cousin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leotychides" title="Leotychides">Leotychides</a>. Sometime after 490 BC, the humiliated Demaratus had chosen to go into exile, and had made his way to Darius's court in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h202_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-h202-89">[89]</a></sup> Demaratus would from then on act as an advisor to Darius, and later Xerxes, on Greek affairs, and accompanied Xerxes during the second Persian invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> At the end of Herodotus's book 7, there is an anecdote relating that prior to the second invasion, Demaratus sent an apparently blank wax tablet to Sparta. When the wax was removed, a message was found scratched on the wooden backing, warning the Spartans of Xerxes's plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> However, many historians believe that this chapter was inserted into the text by a later author, possibly to fill a <a href="/wiki/Lacuna_(manuscripts)" title="Lacuna (manuscripts)">gap</a> between the end of book 7 and the start of book 8. The veracity of this anecdote is therefore unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenic_alliance">Hellenic alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Hellenic alliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 481 BC, Xerxes sent ambassadors to city states throughout Greece, asking for food, land, and water as tokens of their submission to Persia. However, Xerxes' ambassadors deliberately avoided Athens and Sparta, hoping thereby that those states would not learn of the Persians' plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> States that were opposed to Persia thus began to coalesce around these two city states. A congress of states met at <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a> in late autumn of 481 BC, and a confederate alliance of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">Greek city-states</a> was formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII145_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-VII145-118">[118]</a></sup> This confederation had powers both to send envoys to ask for assistance and to dispatch troops from the member states to defensive points after joint consultation. Herodotus does not formulate an abstract name for the union but simply calls them "οἱ Ἕλληνες" (the Greeks) and "the Greeks who had sworn alliance" (Godley translation) or "the Greeks who had banded themselves together" (Rawlinson translation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> From now on, they will be referred to as the 'Allies'. Sparta and Athens had a leading role in the congress but the interests of all the states influenced defensive strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII160_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-VII160-120">[120]</a></sup> Little is known about the internal workings of the congress or the discussions during its meetings. Only 70 of the nearly 700 Greek city-states sent representatives. Nevertheless, this was remarkable for the disjointed Greek world, especially since many of the city-states present were still technically at war with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h226_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-h226-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_invasion_of_Greece_.28480.E2.80.93479_BC.29">Second invasion of Greece (480–479 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greco-Persian_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Second invasion of Greece (480–479 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a></div>
<div aria-labelledby="Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">

invasion of Greece</a></span></div>
